         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301509-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0030-title">2.1 Electrolyte Selection and Optimization</h3>
            
            <p>To ensure the normal function of SIHCs at LT, electrolyte needs to have the following requirements: i) keep liquid state at LT, especially below −30&nbsp;°C; ii) maintain an acceptable ionic conductivity at LT; iii) form thin and stable SEI with low interfacial resistance. First, the concentration of NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte is 1.0&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> according to the viscosity and ionic conductivity of the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). Under this concentration, SIHCs are expected to exhibit excellent LT performance. Second, the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte remained in a liquid state at −60&nbsp;°C, while NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC became frozen at −40&nbsp;°C, as observed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It can be rationally predicted that SIHC using NaOTf–DEGDME could operate at ultralow temperature. These observed trends were further confirmed by comparing the ionic conductivity of three different electrolytes with decreasing temperature measured by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a> (Supporting Information). The NaOTf–DEGDME maintained an impressive ionic conductivity of 0.30 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −70&nbsp;°C, which far exceeds that of NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC (0.038 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C) and NaPF<sub>6</sub>–DEGDME (0.064 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −70&nbsp;°C). Furthermore, the ionic conductivity of NaOTf–DEGDME was almost linear with temperature, while the ionic conductivity of NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC and NaPF<sub>6</sub>–DEGDME electrolytes dropped sharply at −30&nbsp;°C. After fitting the relationship between ionic conductivity and temperature, the conductive activation energy of 0.173&nbsp;eV of the NaOTf–DEGDME could be calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2c</a>), implying fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/50b8db30-86a9-4dfa-8172-9fb1c15e2058/aenm202301509-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/50b8db30-86a9-4dfa-8172-9fb1c15e2058/aenm202301509-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/49dcdae4-dec8-4481-9249-588786d0f540/aenm202301509-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/50b8db30-86a9-4dfa-8172-9fb1c15e2058/aenm202301509-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Properties of electrolytes at different temperatures. a) Photograph of the 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTf–DEGDME at an ultralow temperature of −60&nbsp;°C. b) Ionic conductivity of different electrolytes. c) Electric conductance activation energy of the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte. d) Na<sup>+</sup> radial distribution function, snapshot, and most probable solvation structure extracted from MD simulation of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC electrolyte at 25&nbsp;°C. e,f) Na<sup>+</sup> radial distribution functions, snapshot, and most probable solvation structure extracted from MD simulation of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte at 25 (e) and −40&nbsp;°C (f).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation process is directly related to LT performance. To gain insights into the solvation structure of these two electrolytes, classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2d</a>, the radial distribution function of Na<sup>+</sup> in NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC exhibited a typical solvent-separated ion pair (SSIP) structure, in which Na ions were mainly coordinated by EC/DMC solvent molecules (coordination number of 3.3 EC/DMC oxygen per Na<sup>+</sup> within 3.1 Å), while the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> was outside the solvation shell (coordination number of 0.97 PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> fluorine per Na<sup>+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0002_R_d78737132e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0003_R_d78737132e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the NaOTf–DEGDME (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2e</a>) showed a OTf<sup>−</sup> partially involved in solvation structure with a calculated coordination number of 2.78 per Na<sup>+</sup>. This configuration was similar to the contact ion pair (CIP) structure, which was commonly observed in previous literature involving electrolytes containing high salt concentrations or low-polarizability solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0002_R_d78737132e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This CIP structure in the NaOTf–DEGDME may be ascribed to the high electron affinity of high-polarity ─CF<sub>3</sub> groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0006_R_d78737132e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Experimental evidence of these MD results was obtained from Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra of the electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with pure DEGDME solvent, some characteristic peaks of DEGDME in the NaOTf–DEGDME systems are shifted with the increase of the electrolyte concentration, indicating an obvious interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and DEGDME, in agreement with the CIP solvation structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0002_R_d78737132e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0008_R_d78737132e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sup>+</sup> transference numbers of the NaOTf–DEGDME and NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC at 25&nbsp;°C are measured to be 0.590 and 0.348, respectively, which is ascribed to this CIP solvation structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte also displays a similar CIP structure at LT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0002">2f</a> (−40&nbsp;°C) and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6 and S7</a> (Supporting Information) (0&nbsp;°C)). Therefore, the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte not only displayed the characteristics of LT resistance and high ionic conductivity, but also its weakly solvated structure avoided the cointercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>–solvent, resulting in better structural stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structural Design and Preparation of Anode Material</h3>
            
            <p>In addition to selecting a suitable electrolyte, another key strategy is the rational design of novel anode materials with suitable diffusion kinetics for both Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons to further boost LT performance. What kind of structure is conducive to alleviating the volume expansion during sodiation and promoting the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion? In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3a</a>, three models were built to investigate the stress variation of spherical carbon materials with/without hollow and through-hole structure during sodiation, including solid carbon sphere (SCS, <i>R</i> = 200&nbsp;nm), hollow carbon sphere (HCS, <i>R</i> = 200&nbsp;nm, <i>δ</i> = 25&nbsp;nm), and TH-HCS (<i>R</i> = 200&nbsp;nm, <i>δ</i> = 25&nbsp;nm, <i>R</i><sub>through-hole</sub> = 15&nbsp;nm), where <i>R</i>, <i>δ</i>, and <i>R</i><sub>through-hole</sub> were the radius of a sphere, the thickness, and the radius of through-hole within the shell of TH-HCS, respectively. Due to the inconsistency of the densities of the sodiation product and the original reactant (C<sub>64</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup>&nbsp;→ NaC<sub>64</sub>), the crystal lattice changed during sodiation, resulting in volumetric expansion and the corresponding internal stress. During the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> from the exterior to the interior of the sample, the stress in the SCS grew steadily from the outside to the inside, and the accumulation of internal stresses would cause structural fracture of SCS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3a</a>). In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the maximum stress of the SCS under 25&nbsp;°C after 0.4 s was 0.577&nbsp;MPa, much higher than that of both HCS (0.0244&nbsp;MPa) and TH-HCS (0.0073&nbsp;MPa), indicating that the inner hollow structure could release stresses from the inside out during sodiation, promoting the stability of the carbon sphere over long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0017_R_d78737132e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, compared with hollow structure, through-hole structure could further relieve internal stress. The maximum stress of TH-HCS was lower than that of HCS, and it only concentrated at the around of through-hole. The stress variation of TH-HCS during sodiation at 0&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3b</a>) and −40&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3c</a>)) was also investigated, and these two results were consistent with the result of 25&nbsp;°C, i.e., the maximum stress at LT was mainly concentrated around of through-hole. Therefore, the mechanism of the shell structure to release the concentrated stress was dispersing the generated stress to the inner and outer surfaces, and the construction of the through-hole structure could further alleviate the stress of the concentrated material.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dac00cb1-6c8f-4a92-adf9-1634bd8c9231/aenm202301509-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/dac00cb1-6c8f-4a92-adf9-1634bd8c9231/aenm202301509-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96d22894-c25c-4596-8413-ae111664ff72/aenm202301509-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dac00cb1-6c8f-4a92-adf9-1634bd8c9231/aenm202301509-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural design and the target material analysis. a) Cross-sectional stress contours at 25&nbsp;°C after 0.1 s of solid carbon sphere, hollow carbon sphere, and through-hole hollow carbon sphere. b,c) Cross-sectional stress contours of through-hole hollow carbon sphere at 0&nbsp;°C (b) and −40&nbsp;°C (c) after 0.1 s. d,e) The top and side views of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways in carbon with a small (d) and large (e) defective structure. f,g) Diffusion energy profile for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in carbon with a small (f) and large (g) defective structure. h,i) Scanning electronic microscopy (h) and transmission electron microscopy (i) images of TH-HCS. j) Pore size distribution of HCS and TH-HCS.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The pore structure in carbon materials has an effect on the transport kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition to the aforementioned through-hole and hollow structures that improve the wettability of electrolyte and relieve the internal stress, the defective structure also has a significant effect on the migration kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0017_R_d78737132e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To reveal this effect, the corresponding density functional theory (DFT) calculation was performed. The Na<sup>+</sup> migration energies in two carbon samples with various defect structures were calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). The proposed Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths in these carbons and their corresponding diffusion energy along these paths were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3f,g</a>. The carbon with a larger defective structure exhibited a negative diffusion energy of Na<sup>+</sup> (−0.18&nbsp;eV), while Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in carbon with a small pore structure conquered a large positive energy (+7.05&nbsp;eV). The above comparison showed that Na<sup>+</sup> had a certain spontaneous migration phenomenon in carbon materials with a large defective structure. This unique phenomenon that Na<sup>+</sup> could spontaneously migrate into carbon materials with defective structures has also been reported in recent literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0018_R_d78737132e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0019_R_d78737132e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has been well accepted that the negative diffusion energy barrier endows higher charge mobility, ultimately leading to rapid sodiation kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>Theoretical analysis proved that hollow structure released the stress of carbon material during sodiation, while through-hole structure further relieved the internal stress and promoted good electrolyte permeation, ensuring structural stability and better charge/Na<sup>+</sup> transfer. Additionally, the defective structure further accelerated the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, preparing carbon material with the through-hole hollow and defective structure is expected to exhibit remarkable LT performance. A schematic of the preparation process of this designed TH-HCS via a calcination and etching strategy was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). For comparison, HCS without through-hole structure was prepared by a single-template method, in which the same conditions without adding SiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information), both TH-HCS and HCS exhibited a uniform spherical structure. Besides, TH-HCS possessed abundant through-holes and the surface was rather rough, while HCS displayed a smooth surface. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a> (Supporting Information), both TH-HCS and HCS were similar in diameter (around 420&nbsp;nm) and wall thickness (around 22&nbsp;nm). Additionally, TH-HCS had a through-hole size in the range of 10–50&nbsp;nm on the shell, which was expected to provide more pore channels for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer and good electrolyte accessibility. The size of this through-hole was exactly the difference in the pore size distribution of TH-HCS and HCS. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0003">3j</a>, micropore and macropore areas remained highly uniform in these two samples except for a clear difference in mesopores. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) results for TH-HCS and HCS showed a type-IV model (an H3 hysteresis loop), representing a typical feature of hollow spheres with hierarchical pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0020_R_d78737132e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrated that both TH-HCS and HCS exhibited a high degree of amorphous structure and the layer spacing was larger than that of graphite (JCPDS Card is No. 65–6212),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0021_R_d78737132e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which could alleviate the volume expansion upon the repetition of sodiation. Additionally, high N-doping, especially quaternary-N and pyridinic-N doping, could reduce the adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> and change the electron localization structure, thus obtaining high reversible capacity and good electron conductivity (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14 and S15</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0022_R_d78737132e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be concluded that double-template method could construct a through-hole hollow structure and showed an advantage in introducing abundant edges/defects and increasing specific surface area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), which facilitated the wettability of electrolyte, provided more active sites, and increased ionic/electronic conductivity. Hope that as expected, TH-HCS exhibits remarkable Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0050-title">2.3 Anode/Electrolyte Interface Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The pore structure, especially the through-hole structure, and the type of electrolyte have a great influence on the electrochemical performance of carbon materials. To systematically investigate the structure–activity relationship between structure (with or without through-hole structure)/electrolyte and performance, Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of TH-HCS and HCS were evaluated in NaOTf–DEGDME and NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC electrolytes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4a</a> showed cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The electrochemical behavior of TH-HCS displays the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism of sodium storage in the CV curves. Specifically, the capacity comes from “adsorption” at surface-like active sites (including the large interlayer space, edges, pores, and heteroatoms) above 0.1&nbsp;V. Furthermore, the interlayer distance of ≈0.39&nbsp;nm is large enough for Na<sup>+</sup> “interlayer insertion.” This “insertion” sodium storage mechanism exhibits a plateau range in the discharge/charge curve and a sharp peak in the CV below 0.1&nbsp;V obviously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0023_R_d78737132e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CV curves displayed one pair of redox peaks at low potentials of 0.01 and 0.08&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with highly overlapping profiles of the initial five cycles, suggesting the structure stability of TH-HCS during cycling when using NaOTf–DEGDME. Additionally, the first curve was slightly larger than the subsequent curves, which was caused by the irreversible reaction between electrolyte and TH-HCS, and simultaneously the formation of SEI on the surface of electrode. Although an irreversible reaction occurred at the first discharge process, it could be seen from a slight change that the TH-HCS displayed a very high ICE in the NaOTf–DEGDME. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a> (Supporting Information) displayed the first three galvanostatic discharge/charge (GDC) curves of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and the shape of the sodiation and desodiation curves was consistent with the CV. The high discharge capacities of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at the first, second, and three cycles could reach 530, 441, and 417 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It can be obtained that TH-HCS with through-hole hollow structure using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited a high ICE of 84.5%, which was higher than that using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC (54.4%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a>, Supporting Information) and HCS with only hollow structure using NaOTf–DEGDME (70.2%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c</a>, Supporting Information). Importantly, the ICE of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME was also higher than most previous carbonaceous materials, both in terms of large and low specific surface area (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information)).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0024_R_d78737132e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0045_R_d78737132e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through the comparison of the ICE of TH-HCS and HCS using different electrolytes, it could be concluded that i) electrolyte had a greater effect on ICE enhancement. As mentioned above, the role of electrolytes was closely related to the formation of SEI. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was employed to obtain the morphology of SEI of two electrodes using different electrolytes. The SEI in both TH-HCS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4c</a>) and HCS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited thinness and homogeneity, while it in TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC was thick and inhomogeneous (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4f</a>). Thus, using NaOTf–DEGDME for carbon materials was an effective way for forming a uniform and thin SEI. ii) The construction of through-hole structure could further improve ICE. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information), TH-HCS had better wettability for NaOTf–DEGDME compared to HCS. Additionally, the excellent Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics promotes to some extent the faster formation of thin and stable SEI, which can inhibit the further decomposition of electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0018_R_d78737132e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0036_R_d78737132e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0037_R_d78737132e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the through-hole structure was conducive to the wetting of electrolyte and thus improved the ICE of TH-HCS.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4073e78-71ef-4721-833b-2af08742f205/aenm202301509-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4073e78-71ef-4721-833b-2af08742f205/aenm202301509-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4392ff19-f03d-4582-99b4-4bb1e0839b88/aenm202301509-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4073e78-71ef-4721-833b-2af08742f205/aenm202301509-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The anode/electrolyte interface analysis. a) CV curves of TH-HCS in the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> under 25&nbsp;°C. b) Comparison of ICE of TH-HCS and HCS in two electrolytes and some previous similar works. c,f) TEM images of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME (c) and NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC electrolyte (f) after five cycles. d,e) The high-resolution XPS spectrum of F 1s (d) and C 1s (e) of TH-HCS after five cycles in the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte. g,h) The high-resolution XPS spectrum of F 1s (g) and C 1s (h) of TH-HCS after five cycles in NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC. i) Young's modulus mappings and energy dissipation mappings of TH-HCS using different electrolytes after 5 and 50 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A thin and homogeneous SEI was important for both high ICE and the fast migration of Na<sup>+</sup>. The migration kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> was closely related to the composition of SEI. Therefore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra were employed to investigate the compositions of the SEI of TH-HCS using two electrolytes. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), comparison of the F 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4d,g</a>) indicated two main types of fluorine-based components: i) Na─F (≈684.1&nbsp;eV) and ─CF<sub>2</sub> (≈688.1&nbsp;eV), common to both, which was usually a product of the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>/NaOTf and binder, respectively;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0046_R_d78737132e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Storage Mater.">46</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0047_R_d78737132e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Environ. Sci.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ii) P─F (≈687.7&nbsp;eV) and ─CF<sub>3</sub> (≈689.2&nbsp;eV), unique to both, from the salt of two electrolytes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0047_R_d78737132e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Environ. Sci.">47</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0048_R_d78737132e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NaOTf–DEGDME with electron-withdrawing ─CF<sub>3</sub> groups suppressed Na<sup>+</sup> solvation and enables efficient NaF formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0006_R_d78737132e1164" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, both SEI possessed a high Na─F component, which was derived from a F-containing salt that effectively stabilized the interface between electrolyte and electrode, thereby enhancing the cycle stability and fast charging capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0003_R_d78737132e1170" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0007_R_d78737132e1170" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In fact, this is the reason why a NaF-rich SEI has been built by various strategies recently. Unfortunately, as observed in the comparison of C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4e,h</a>), an increased peak characteristic of O─C═O (≈534.4&nbsp;eV) was displayed in TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC, which was mainly attributed to ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The presence of abundant carbonates with poor mechanical strength in SEI led to sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0011_R_d78737132e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the presence of Na─S in SEI could also promote the Na<sup>+</sup> migration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). More importantly, the composition of SEI on the surface of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME did not change significantly after longer cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information), proving the durability of the SEI. Hence, the NaF-rich and low-carbonate composition of SEI contributed to high rate and long-cycling life of TH-HCS at ultralow temperatures.</p>
            
            <p>The mechanical properties of SEI, which are closely related to the mechanical stability against the volume expansion, play important roles in the electrochemical behaviors of electrodes. To gain insight into the mechanical properties of the SEI on the TH-HCS using different electrolytes, atomic force microscopy (AFM) was employed. Compared with the SEI on the TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC with a distinct undulating morphology, the SEI on the TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME was uniformly stacked and maintained no obvious concave and bulge morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the 3D Young's modulus plots of the SEI after 5 and 50 cycles showed that the modulus of the SEI on the TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME was more robust than that using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4i</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information)), which could alleviate the stress caused by the volume expansion of TH-HCS during cycling and thus inhibited the break of the SEI. A similar conclusion about the formation of tough SEI in ether electrolytes can also be obtained from the energy dissipation mappings. The whole mechanical work done by the AFM probe during the loading process can be divided into energy dissipation and elastic recovery energy during the following unloading process. It is known from the literature that energy dissipation is closely related to the microcrack formation and plastic deformation of the sample during loading–unloading.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0049_R_d78737132e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0004">4i</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the smaller energy dissipation of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME indicated that the ether electrolyte enabled a more robust SEI with less irreversible deformation of the electrode during the repetition of sodiation. The stabler SEI on TH-HCS largely avoided further decomposition of the NaOTf–DEGDME, thus reducing the amount of active Na<sup>+</sup> consumed to form a new SEI, resulting in excellent cycling performance. The above three comparisons directly revealed that NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte inspired a robust and well-distributed SEI on the TH-HCS.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0060-title">2.4 Room-Temperature Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To analyze LT Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties and mechanisms of TH-HCS, it is first necessary to understand these at room-temperature. The rate performance was shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5a</a>. For TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME, remarkable reversible capacities of 413, 323, 281, 256, 240, and 228 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be obtained at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, a high discharge capacity of 223 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained even at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, showing great merit for abuse tolerance with varied current densities. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), the rate retention of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at different current densities was always higher than that of HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME and TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC, which was ascribed to the weakly solvated structure, the thin SEI, and the construction of a through-hole structure. Besides, TH-HCS exhibited a high reversible capacity of 463 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 60 cycles when the current density backs to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which was higher than at the first several cycles. This particular phenomenon might be related to the activation of electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0026_R_d78737132e1269" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b94387e1-98b8-4f3b-874a-6523e6eed965/aenm202301509-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b94387e1-98b8-4f3b-874a-6523e6eed965/aenm202301509-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/43d088a6-ea6d-425c-91a5-01ecfd55e9e7/aenm202301509-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b94387e1-98b8-4f3b-874a-6523e6eed965/aenm202301509-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of TH-HCS at room temperature. a) Rate performance. b) Cycling property of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c,d) Cycling life at 1.0 (c) and 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (d). e) Comparison of cycling property of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME and other carbonaceous materials at a high current density. f) <i>σ</i>-values. g) <i>b</i>-values. h) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient during sodiation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5b</a> displayed the cycling stability of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME showed the most cycling stability, and after undergoing an initial activation procedure, the reversible capacity reached 480 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and remained at 472 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even up to 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with CE close to 100%. This phenomenon of falling first and rising later also appeared in some related works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0026_R_d78737132e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0030_R_d78737132e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Environ. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC and HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME only showed low capacities of 189 and 177 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5c</a>, TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited a remarkable cyclability with a high reversible capacity of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which was obviously higher than that of TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC (122 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME (137 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). After 10 cycles of activation at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME maintained a superior capacity of 206.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> even after 4000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5d</a>). The cycle property of previous carbonaceous materials was shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5e</a>, further indicating that TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited outstanding cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0036_R_d78737132e1360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0050_R_d78737132e1360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">50</a>-<a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0055_R_d78737132e1360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In order to reveal the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of TH-HCS, relevant systematic analysis is necessary. First, the EIS curves are very helpful in analyzing the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer resistance in SEI and electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). To quantitatively investigate the electrochemical processes of HCS and TH-HCS, EIS curves at 2.0&nbsp;V ((pristine (2.0&nbsp;V) and charge to 2.0&nbsp;V)) were fitted with the equivalent circuit displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). The TH-HCS and HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited lower SEI layer resistance and charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) than TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC and remained highly consistent at 2.0&nbsp;V, indicating that: i) SEI with higher ionic conductivity was formed in NaOTf–DEGDME; ii) the construction of through-hole structure facilitated the Na<sup>+</sup> transport; iii) the construction of the through-hole structure and solid SEI contributed to the stability of electrodes. Besides, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of TH-HCS and HCS using different electrolytes was calculated as detailed in the Supporting Information. Using the same NaOTf–DEGDME, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of TH-HCS was larger than that of HCS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5f</a>), proving that the through-hole structure was beneficial to the Na<sup>+</sup> transmission. Based on these two aspects, carbon with the through-hole hollow structure using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information) depicted the sweep CV of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME. Perceptibly, the redox peak increased with the increasing scan rate during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior about two contributions (diffusion behavior and pseudocapacitive behavior) of TH-HCS and HCS using different electrolytes can be accurately obtained. The comparison showed that Na<sup>+</sup> storage reaction of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME was mainly controlled by pseudocapacitive behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5g</a>). The pseudocapacitive behavior played an important role in fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics, which was ascribed to the synergistic effect of through-hole structure and NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte. Quantitatively, as the scan rate increased from 0.2 to 10.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), the pseudocapacitance proportion of the total capacity of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME gradually enhanced from 51% to 88%, which was significantly higher than that of TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC and HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at identical scan rates, implying that the matching of through-hole hollow structure and electrolyte could enhance the capacitive contribution. This conclusion explains why TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited superior rate performance. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was employed to further reveal the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> transmission kinetics of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME. The voltage response of TH-HCS and HCS using different electrolytes was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5h</a>, all of them showed a decreased <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> with the discharge proceeding and TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited the fastest <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> than HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME and TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC between the voltage of 0.3 and 2.5&nbsp;V, while showing similar <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> with two others between the voltage of 0.01 and 0.3&nbsp;V. This phenomenon was ascribed to the different storage mechanisms at different voltages, where Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior at a low potential was mainly controlled by diffusion–intercalation mechanism, while it at high potential was controlled by the adsorption mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0020_R_d78737132e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combined with the sweep CV results, it can be demonstrated that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage reaction of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME was mainly controlled by the adsorption mechanism. All of CV, GITT, and EIS results confirm that TH-HCS anode exhibits the fastest kinetics in the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0070-title">2.5 Low-Temperature Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Behaviors</h3>
            
            <p>In addition to the outstanding room-temperature performance, advanced LT performances were also significant for practical applications in high-altitude or cold climate areas. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information), TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited high ICE of 79.4%, 80.3%, and 76.0% at 0, −40, and −60&nbsp;°C, respectively, which were very similar to that at 25&nbsp;°C and much higher than that using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC. Similar to the room-temperature results, the high ICE correlates with thin SEI generated using NaOTf–DEGDME. In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6a</a>, the TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME also exhibited high reversible capacities of 210 (153 and 129), 190 (134 and 112), 176 (126 and 101), 165 (113 and 89), 157 (104 and 69), 149 (100 and 55), and 141 (92 and 41) mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0&nbsp;°C (−40 and −60&nbsp;°C) and the current densities from 0.05 to 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, a high capacity of TH-HCS could be reached 195, 132, and 116 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 60 cycles when the current density backs to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> at 0, −40, and −60&nbsp;°C, respectively. Compared with rate performance at 25&nbsp;°C, the TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited higher retention of Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity at −40&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information). Similarly, the TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME exhibited high cycling performance at LT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6b</a>). A high reversible capacity of 143.5, 93.9, and 58.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be maintained at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 0, −40, and −60&nbsp;°C, respectively. Furthermore, TH-HCS delivered an ultrahigh capacity of 140.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of ≈82.3% over 11&nbsp;000 cycles at 0&nbsp;°C and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6c</a>). When the temperature drops to −40&nbsp;°C, a reversible capacity of 87.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be still maintained over 11&nbsp;000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the capacity attenuation of each cycle being ≈0.001%, further indicating its superior LT cycling life. In comparison, TH-HCS using NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC displayed unsatisfactory capacity, low-rate, and short-cycling performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e3c3684e-d1c1-4149-96f6-6164e2a537a0/aenm202301509-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e3c3684e-d1c1-4149-96f6-6164e2a537a0/aenm202301509-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6313788-a104-4b3d-aeb7-859e72392f1d/aenm202301509-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e3c3684e-d1c1-4149-96f6-6164e2a537a0/aenm202301509-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of TH-HCS at low temperature. a) Rate performance at three low temperatures. b,c) Long-cycling performance at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) <i>b</i>-values. e) Capacitive contribution at different scan rates. f) GITT profiles and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient during the discharging process (inset). g) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion stability. h) <i>σ</i>-values at different temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Sweep CV, GITT, and EIS are also employed to investigate Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and transmission kinetics in TH-HCS at LT. According to sweep CV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information), it could be observed that <i>b</i>-value increased along with the decrease of temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6d</a>), and the surface pseudocapacitance proportion of overall capacity of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in TH-HCS at −40&nbsp;°C were higher than those at 25 and 0&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6e</a>) after calculating, indicating that the proportion of surface adsorption mechanism to the specific capacity increased with decreasing temperature. For instance, the surface pseudocapacitance proportion of overall capacity at 10.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> gradually enhanced from 88% to 94% when the temperature decreased from 25 to −40&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a>, Supporting Information). This result is in accordance with the rate performance of TH-HCS using NaOTf–DEGDME at 25, 0, and −40&nbsp;°C, that is to say, the capacity retention of TH-HCS using this electrolyte at LT, especially at −40&nbsp;°C, exhibited higher than that at 25&nbsp;°C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6f</a> showed GITT profiles of TH-HCS during the discharging process at LT and the corresponding <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>. Compared with <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of TH-HCS at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0005">5h</a>), although <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> slowed down as the temperature dropped, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at LT was more stable than that at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6g</a>), that is, TH-HCS could always maintain a stable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate during the charge/discharge process, which was benefited from the adsorption mechanism. This phenomenon could also be obtained from EIS analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0006">6h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a> (Supporting Information)).</p>
            
            <p>Based on the systematic analysis, such a superior performance intimately corresponds to the optimized anode structure and suitable weakly solvated electrolyte, in which through-hole structure offered short channels for fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer, promoted impregnation of electrolyte, and adapted 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte had a greater effect on ICE enhancement and consistently maintained the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transmission over a wide range of temperatures, especially at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0056_R_d78737132e1607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nat. Commun.">56</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0057_R_d78737132e1607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such LT performance combined with the abovementioned room-temperature properties further clears the obstacles for the practical application of TH-HCS.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202301509-sec-0080-title">2.6 Full-Cell Performance at Different Temperatures</h3>
            
            <p>To enlarge the commercial application of TH-HCS, a LT SIHC matched AC (TH-HCS||AC) was assembled. To the best of our knowledge, no literature has been reported for such LT SIHC. The electrochemical property of AC in half-cell was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a> (Supporting Information) and a typical double-layer capacitance mechanism could be observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0013_R_d78737132e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a> (Supporting Information), linear sweep voltammetry measurements demonstrated that the NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte exhibited high anodic stability up to 4.08&nbsp;V, implying its potential usability for SIHCs with high energy density.</p>
            
            <p>The schematic illustration of TH-HCS||AC SIHC was shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7a</a>. The anions of [SO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub>]<sup>−</sup> and the cations of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charging process are absorbed in the surface of AC and TH-HCS, respectively. The discharge process is vice versa. Importantly, TH-HCS and AC electrode need to be presodiation in their respective half-cells before SIHC assembling, which can decrease initial unnecessary capacity loss. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7b</a>, a TH-HCS||AC SIHC lighted up light-emitting diode (LED) bulbs at different temperatures, confirming its auspicious application. This SIHC exhibited a large working potential (0.01–3.8&nbsp;V). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7c–e</a> showed CV curves of TH-HCS||AC SIHC at 25, 0, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. Similar CV curves with no obvious redox peak indicated that Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior, which was mainly controlled by the capacitive mechanism, has not obviously changed. This result could also be obtained from GDC curves at three temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> (Supporting Information)). The capacity of the TH-HCS||AC SIHC is calculated based on the mass of the whole anode and cathode materials. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7g</a>, TH-HCS||AC SIHC displayed high capacities of 96, 77, and 62 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05, 0.5, and 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, when the temperature dropped to −40&nbsp;°C, superior capacities of 44, 19, and 13 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were still maintained at identical current densities. The significant rate capability was closely related to the suitable electrolyte and the fast kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> in the cathode/anode materials. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7h</a>, the Ragone plots were tally up the energy/power density of TH-HCS||AC SIHC. At the same power density of 409&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, TH-HCS||AC SIHC exhibited a high energy density of 106.1, 76.1, and 52.0&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25, 0, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. When the power density increased to 8190&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, a high energy density of 46.6, 28.6, and 11.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> could be still reached at these low temperatures. These outstanding performances are better than representative hybrid capacitors and most batteries reported in literature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7i</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0013_R_d78737132e1692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0058_R_d78737132e1692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nano Energy">58</a>, <a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0059_R_d78737132e1692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Storage Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After 5000 cycles, TH-HCS||AC SIHC exhibited high-capacity retention of 81.1%, 75.3%, and 57.7% at 25, 0, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202301509-fig-0007">7j</a>), confirming a desired potential application with long cycling life, high energy density, and large power density. Additionally, the capacity loss decreased with decreasing temperature between 5000 cycles, proving that the TH-HCS structure was more stable at LT.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202301509-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a6b3522-63e6-4a1e-a5cd-1189a55add3f/aenm202301509-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a6b3522-63e6-4a1e-a5cd-1189a55add3f/aenm202301509-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c25aa10f-b5f2-4dc3-a7bb-ee2f18598d7e/aenm202301509-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a6b3522-63e6-4a1e-a5cd-1189a55add3f/aenm202301509-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202301509-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202301509" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical property of TH-HCS||AC SIHC using NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte. a) Schematic illustration. b) Photographs of LED lighted via TH-HCS||AC SIHC at different temperatures. c–e) CV curves at 25 (c), 0 (d), and −40&nbsp;°C (e). f) GDC curves at 0&nbsp;°C. g) Rate performance. h) Ragone plots of TH-HCS||AC SIHC at different temperatures. i) Ragone plots of TH-HCS||AC SIHC with other energy storage equipment. j) Cycling performance at different temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301509-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we developed an ether electrolyte with a single solute and a single solvent and constructed a hollow carbon sphere with a through-hole structure for SIHCs, and revealed that their synergistic effect enabled SIHCs to simultaneously exhibit high-capacity retention, remarkable stability, and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics at ultralow temperature to support high energy/power density and long cycling life. Specifically, employing TH-HCS and 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTf–DEGDME electrolyte yielded a high ICE of 84.5% and a specific capacity of 206.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 4000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 25&nbsp;°C, 87.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 11&nbsp;000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and −40&nbsp;°C, and 58.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and −60&nbsp;°C. As expected, at 409&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, TH-HCS assembled with AC to construct a SIHC exhibited a remarkable energy density of 106.1, 76.1, and 52.0&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25, 0, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. This remarkable LT performance was attributed to the LT resistance of electrolyte with weakly solvated structure, the through-hole hollow structure of anode, and the highly fluorinated SEI. This work provides an early route to enabling excellent comprehensive properties of energy storage devices at ultralow temperatures.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202301509-sec-0100-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0110">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0120">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0130-title"> Synthesis of TH-HCS</h4>
                  
                  <p>The classic Stöber approach was employed to synthesize the colloidal silica. The diameter of colloidal silica was ≈350&nbsp;nm. Then, 6.0&nbsp;mL of ammonia solution (NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, 28.0&nbsp;wt%) was added into a mixture solution of 17.0&nbsp;mL of ethanol and 25.0&nbsp;mL of deionization water with ethanolic solution of ethyl silicate (TEOS). The ethanolic solution of TEOS was obtained by adding 2.3–45.0&nbsp;mL of ethanol. After that, the mixture solution was dispersed in the isopropanol (60.0&nbsp;mL) and then the (3-aminopropyl)triethoxysilane (0.2&nbsp;mL) was added into the above solution. This reaction process under ultrasound at 80&nbsp;°C needed to be carried out continuously for 2.0 h. The SiO<sub>2</sub>─NH<sub>2</sub> powder was obtained after a “centrifugation, washing, and freeze-drying” process. Then, 50.0&nbsp;mg of SiO<sub>2</sub>─NH<sub>2</sub> was added into 12.0&nbsp;mL of ethanol under ultrasound. And 100.0&nbsp;mg of dopamine and 9.0&nbsp;mg SiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with a diameter of ≈25&nbsp;nm dispersed in deionization water (18.0&nbsp;mL) were dispersed into mixture. After 30.0&nbsp;min, 0.5&nbsp;mL of ammonia solution was added. After standing for 24 h, the obtained precursor was treated at 350&nbsp;°C for 3.0 h, and then rising the temperature to 800&nbsp;°C. Subsequently, the SiO<sub>2</sub>@C/SiO<sub>2</sub> could be obtained after 2.0 h. The SiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and SiO<sub>2</sub>─NH<sub>2</sub> were etched by 4.0&nbsp;wt% HF aqueous solution. Finally, the TH-HCS was obtained after washing and drying method.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0140-title"> Physical Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD patterns were characterized by D8 ADVANCE with Cu Kα radiation at 40&nbsp;kV and 20&nbsp;mA. A LabRAM HR Evolution spectrograph with a He/Ne laser of <i>λ</i> = 532&nbsp;nm (&lt;10&nbsp;mW, Horiba Scientific, Longjumeau) was employed to obtained Raman spectroscopy. Scanning electronic microscopy and TEM images were observed using an Ultra 55 microscope (Germany) operated at 3.0&nbsp;kV and a Tecnai G2 20 TWIN (FEI) microscope (USA) operated at 200&nbsp;kV, respectively. Thermo Scientific K-Alpha+ was employed to obtain XPS spectra. The porosity and BET surface areas were obtained using a N<sub>2</sub> sorption tool (ASAP2460, Micromeritics). FT-IR was performed using a NICOLET 6700. The atomic force microscopy information was obtained by Bruker Dimension ICON.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0150-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrochemical properties were carried out by using 2032-type batteries. The batteries were placed in the DW-HL398 ultralow temperature freezer box (Zhongke Meiling Cryogenic Technology Co., Ltd.) to obtain low-temperature performance. The electrodes were prepared by mixing the TH-HCS/HCS, polyvinylidene difluoride, and carbon black in a ratio of 8:1:1 by mass ratio in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidinone solvent. The composite slurry was coated on Cu foil. After being dried at 60&nbsp;°C for 12 h, the electrode sheets were rolled by a rolling mill and cut into round pieces with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm. The mass loading of active material was ≈1.7&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The battery assembly was carried out within an Ar-filled glove box (MIKROUNA, China), where the contents of both O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O were below 0.1&nbsp;ppm. Before being transferred to a glove box, the electrodes were dried at 80&nbsp;°C in a vacuum oven for 24 h. Na metal was used as the reference electrode; the electrolyte was 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC (1:1 by volume ratio), 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTf (Aladdin)–DEGDME (Acros, Extra Dry), or 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>–DEGDME; a glass fiber filter (Whatman GF/D) was used as the separator. CV and EIS were measured using a Gamry Reference 3000 (Gamry Co., USA). Cycle performance was conducted on LAND CT2001A/CT3001A battery test system (Wuhan Jinnuo Electronics, Ltd.). The ionic conductivity, the transference number of electrolytes, GITT/SIHC measurements, and EIS/sweep CV analysis were shown in the Supporting Information.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0160-title"> Finite Element Method</h4>
                  
                  <p>Finite element analysis was preformed through COMSOL Multiphysics 5.6. A solid carbon sphere, a hollow carbon, and a through-hole hollow carbon sphere were modeled. Two COMSOL preset interfaces were used to simulate the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> and stress, which were coupled with hygroswelling expansion. The external boundary and the internal region were set Na<sup>+</sup> concentration of 1.0 and 0&nbsp;mol m<sup>−3</sup>, respectively. During the sodiation process, migration of Na<sup>+</sup> from the outside to the interior of the sample caused volumetric expansion and thus led to the corresponding stresses. Assuming 1.0&nbsp;mol Na<sup>+</sup> diffused into C<sub>64</sub>, causing 1.0&nbsp;mol C<sub>64</sub> (volume: 3.42 × 10<sup>−4</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) transformed into NaC<sub>64</sub> (volume: 4.28 × 10<sup>−4</sup> m<sup>3</sup>). The hygroswelling strain was evaluated as: <i>∈</i><sub>hg</sub>  =  β(<i>C</i>  −  <i>C</i><sub>ref</sub>), where <i>∈</i><sub>hg</sub> is the hygroswelling strain and <i>C</i> is the ion concentration. <i>β</i> is the linear hygroswelling coefficient and here is 0.86 × 10<sup>−4</sup> m<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup>. The simulation was done with COMSOL default finer-level mesh and time-dependent direct solver. The mesh optimization and discretization order optimization were both operated.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0170-title"> DFT Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>All structures were optimized in Vienna Ab initio Simulations Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0060_R_d78737132e1901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Phys. Rev. B">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange-correlation functional was in the type of Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof of formalism with the generalized gradient approximation. The projector augmented-wave method was used to describe electron–ion interaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0061_R_d78737132e1906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Phys. Rev. B">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The supercell of 4 × 8 × 1 was used for all the calculations. The energy cutoff was set to 520&nbsp;eV. The Hellmann–Feynman force was set at less than 0.01&nbsp;eV during the ionic relaxation loop. The energy convergence criterion of the electronic self-consistency was set to 10<sup>−7</sup>&nbsp;eV. The vacuum layer of 30 Å was applied to eliminate the layer interaction. Monkhorst–Pack<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0062_R_d78737132e1914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Phys. Rev. B">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>K</i>-mesh of 5 × 3 × 1 was used in the first Brillouin zone. The climbing image nudged elastic band method<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0063_R_d78737132e1921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Chem. Phys.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to evaluate the diffusion ability, and seven transition states were chosen in the whole processes. The relative energy was calculated by the formula: <i>E</i> = <i>E</i><sub>total</sub> − <i>E</i><sub>graphene</sub> − <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202301509-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202301509-sec-0180-title"> Simulation Details and Methodology</h4>
                  
                  <p>Classical MD simulations were carried out in LAMMPS using polymer consistent force field (PCFF) forcefield.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0064_R_d78737132e1948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Comput. Phys. Commun.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charges of neutral solvent molecules (EC, DMC, and DEGDME) were set by PCFF forcefield. The charges of CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> were calculated as Mulliken charge by DFT through VASP. The number ratio of solute and solvent molecules in 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>–EC/DMC and 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>–DEGDME was 1:6.45 and 1:7, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). These two initial structures were randomly generated and an energy minimization was first employed to relax the simulation box. An anneal between 300 and 800 K was performed for 10 cycles within 1.0&nbsp;ns under isothermal–isobaric (<i>NPT</i>) ensemble to achieve a full relaxation. Then, an <i>NPT</i> ensemble simulation was performed for 1.0&nbsp;ns to reach a stable cell. Finally, a canonical (<i>NVT</i>) integrated simulation was performed for another 1.0&nbsp;ns and radius distribution function (RDF) was collected every 0.1&nbsp;ns. These RDFs were all checked to reach a stable state. The external pressure was 1 × 10<sup>5</sup> Pa. The constant temperature and constant pressure were both Berendsen type with decay constant 1.0&nbsp;ps. The time step was set 1.0 fs. The open-source visualization software OVITO<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202301509-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202301509-bib-0065_R_d78737132e1985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Modell. Simul. Mater. Sci. Eng.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to analyze the simulation outputs.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="aenm202301509-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span><span class="author">N. Zhang</span>, <span class="author">T. Deng</span>, <span class="author">S.
